LangChain: Memory¶

In LangChain, "memory" refers to the capability of storing and retrieving information about past interactions within a conversation. This allows a system to reference previous exchanges, thereby maintaining context and coherence over time. Memory in LangChain is essential for creating conversational agents that can remember user inputs and previous AI responses. The memory system can be simple, like a list of recent messages, or complex, involving sophisticated data structures and algorithms to store and query the conversation history.

For more details, you can visit the LangChain documentation.

Outline¶

LangChain provides four types of memory mechanisms to handle conversation history differently based on the needs of your application:

  1. ConversationBufferMemory: This is the simplest form of memory. It stores the entire conversation history as a buffer of text, allowing the LLM to access the full context of the conversation at any point. However, as the conversation grows, the buffer can become quite large, which might increase costs due to the number of tokens processed.

  2. ConversationBufferWindowMemory: Instead of storing the entire conversation, this memory only retains the last K interactions. This creates a sliding window of context, helping to manage token usage more effectively while still maintaining recent conversation relevance.

  3. ConversationTokenBufferMemory: This type of memory is token-based rather than interaction-based. It keeps the conversation history within a specified token limit. Once the limit is reached, older parts of the conversation are flushed. This is useful for ensuring that the context remains within the token limit, which can help control costs and stay within model constraints.

  4. ConversationSummaryMemory: This memory type periodically summarizes older parts of the conversation. It combines a buffer for recent interactions with a summary of the earlier conversation, allowing the LLM to maintain context without storing all the details, thus reducing token usage and maintaining conversational coherence over longer interactions.

  5. Additional Memory Types

    • Vector data memory: Stores text (from conversation or elsewhere) in a vector database and retrieves the most relevant blocks of text.
    • Entity memories: Using an LLM, it remembers details about specific entities.

You can also use multiple memories at one time. E.g., Conversation memory + Entity memory to recall individuals.

You can also store the conversation in a conventional database (such as key-value store or SQL)

What is Vector Data Memory?¶

This memory type stores conversation text or other relevant data in a vector database. It retrieves the most relevant blocks of text based on the context of the ongoing conversation. By converting text into vector representations, it enables efficient semantic search and retrieval, allowing the LLM to access contextually relevant information even if the exact wording doesn't match.

Use this when your application requires retrieving and referencing specific information from a large dataset, such as a knowledge base, past conversation logs, or external documents. This is particularly useful in scenarios where precise contextual information is needed over a broad scope of data.

What is entity memory?¶

Entity Memories are more of an enhanced memory management feature powered by an LLM rather than a standalone database or a separate LLM. They use the capabilities of the LLM to process and recall information about entities dynamically, often storing this information in an internal data structure that is accessed and updated as needed during the conversation. However, the underlying storage can still rely on databases or other persistent storage systems to maintain long-term memory across sessions.

Use this when your application needs to remember and leverage information about specific entities across conversations. For instance, a customer service bot that remembers a user’s preferences or a sales assistant that recalls details about a particular product or client.

In [2]:
# account for deprecation of LLM model
import datetime
# Get the current date
current_date = datetime.datetime.now().date()

# Define the date after which the model should be set to "gpt-3.5-turbo"
target_date = datetime.date(2024, 6, 12)

# Set the model variable based on the current date
if current_date > target_date:
    llm_model = "gpt-3.5-turbo"
else:
    llm_model = "gpt-3.5-turbo-0301"
In [3]:
from langchain.chat_models import ChatOpenAI
from langchain.chains import ConversationChain
from langchain.memory import ConversationBufferMemory
In [4]:
llm = ChatOpenAI(temperature=0.0, model=llm_model)
# Stores the conversation history
memory = ConversationBufferMemory()

# Links the LLM and memory, allowing the AI to have contextual conversations.
conversation = ConversationChain(
    llm=llm, 
    memory = memory,
    verbose=True
)
In [5]:
conversation.predict(input="Hi, my name is Andrew")

{'history': "Human: Hi\nAI: What's up\nHuman: Not much, just hanging\nAI: Cool"}

When using a large language model (LLM) for a chat conversation, the LLM itself is stateless—it doesn't remember previous interactions. Each call to the LLM API is independent and doesn't inherently carry any memory of prior exchanges. To give the appearance of memory, developers explicitly pass the conversation history as context with each new API call.

As conversations with an LLM grow longer, the amount of memory needed increases, leading to higher costs since LLMs charge based on the number of tokens processed. To manage this efficiently, LangChain offers different types of memory mechanisms to store and manage conversation history effectively, helping balance between cost and context relevance. These memory options ensure that you can maintain conversational context without unnecessarily inflating token usage and costs.

ConversationBufferWindowMemory¶

In [19]:
from langchain.memory import ConversationBufferWindowMemory
In [20]:
# K=1 which meants it will only remember one last conversation
memory = ConversationBufferWindowMemory(k=1)
In [21]:
memory.save_context({"input": "Hi"},
                    {"output": "What's up"})
memory.save_context({"input": "Not much, just hanging"},
                    {"output": "Cool"})
In [22]:
memory.load_memory_variables({})
Out[22]:
{'history': 'Human: Not much, just hanging\nAI: Cool'}
In [23]:
llm = ChatOpenAI(temperature=0.0, model=llm_model)
memory = ConversationBufferWindowMemory(k=1)
conversation = ConversationChain(
    llm=llm, 
    memory = memory,
    verbose=False
)
In [24]:
conversation.predict(input="Hi, my name is Andrew")
Out[24]:
"Hello Andrew! It's nice to meet you. How can I assist you today?"
In [25]:
conversation.predict(input="What is 1+1?")
Out[25]:
'1+1 equals 2. Is there anything else you would like to know?'
In [26]:
conversation.predict(input="What is my name?")
Out[26]:
"I'm sorry, I do not have access to personal information such as your name. Is there anything else you would like to know?"

ConversationTokenBufferMemory¶

In [27]:
#!pip install tiktoken
In [28]:
from langchain.memory import ConversationTokenBufferMemory
from langchain.llms import OpenAI
llm = ChatOpenAI(temperature=0.0, model=llm_model)
In [29]:
# Different LLMs use different way to count tokens, thus we need to pass LLM to it
memory = ConversationTokenBufferMemory(llm=llm, max_token_limit=50)
memory.save_context({"input": "AI is what?!"},
                    {"output": "Amazing!"})
memory.save_context({"input": "Backpropagation is what?"},
                    {"output": "Beautiful!"})
memory.save_context({"input": "Chatbots are what?"}, 
                    {"output": "Charming!"})
In [30]:
memory.load_memory_variables({})
Out[30]:
{'history': 'AI: Amazing!\nHuman: Backpropagation is what?\nAI: Beautiful!\nHuman: Chatbots are what?\nAI: Charming!'}

ConversationSummaryMemory¶

In [31]:
from langchain.memory import ConversationSummaryBufferMemory
In [32]:
# create a long string
schedule = "There is a meeting at 8am with your product team. \
You will need your powerpoint presentation prepared. \
9am-12pm have time to work on your LangChain \
project which will go quickly because Langchain is such a powerful tool. \
At Noon, lunch at the italian resturant with a customer who is driving \
from over an hour away to meet you to understand the latest in AI. \
Be sure to bring your laptop to show the latest LLM demo."

memory = ConversationSummaryBufferMemory(llm=llm, max_token_limit=100)
memory.save_context({"input": "Hello"}, {"output": "What's up"})
memory.save_context({"input": "Not much, just hanging"},
                    {"output": "Cool"})
memory.save_context({"input": "What is on the schedule today?"}, 
                    {"output": f"{schedule}"})
In [33]:
memory.load_memory_variables({})
Out[33]:
{'history': "System: The human and AI exchange greetings and discuss the day's schedule. The AI informs the human of a morning meeting with the product team, work on the LangChain project, and a lunch meeting with a customer interested in AI. The AI emphasizes the importance of being prepared for the day's events."}
In [34]:
conversation = ConversationChain(
    llm=llm, 
    memory = memory,
    verbose=True
)
In [35]:
conversation.predict(input="What would be a good demo to show?")

The AI informs the human of a morning meeting with the product team, work on the LangChain project, and a lunch meeting with a customer interested in AI. The AI emphasizes the importance of being prepared for the day's events.
Human: What would be a good demo to show?
AI:

> Finished chain.

It's important to tailor the demos to each audience to make a strong impact."
